Defining Fajr and Dhuhr Prayer Times: The Distinction Between True and False Dawn
Ruling on prayer times with commentary and hadith proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definitions of Fajr and Dhuhr prayer times, identify the true dawn (al-subh al-mu'tarid) from the false dawn, and apply the hadith proof to justify the start of Fajr.

Core Idea
Fajr begins at true horizontal dawn, not false vertical light; Dhuhr starts at zawal and ends when shadows double.

This lesson opens the Book of Prayer from 'Umda al-Ri'aya, a Hanafi commentary. The base text establishes the foundational times for Fajr and Dhuhr prayers. Understanding these definitions is crucial because praying outside the correct time invalidates the prayer. The commentary provides a detailed explanation of the two types of dawn, supported by a hadith from Muslim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling for Fajr and Dhuhr
Read the matn: 'The time for Fajr is from the horizontal dawn (true dawn) until sunrise, and for Dhuhr from its decline (zawal) until the shadow of everything becomes twice its length, excluding the shadow of zenith.' This states both the start and end times.
2
Distinguish true dawn from false dawn
The commentator explains that 'al-mu'tarid' (horizontal) excludes 'al-mustatil' (elongated). False dawn appears as a vertical pillar of light followed by darkness; true dawn spreads horizontally and increases gradually.
False dawn is also called 'al-subh al-kadhib' and occurs before true dawn.
3
Examine the hadith proof
The author cites the Prophet's saying: 'Do not let the elongated dawn deceive you, rather the dawn is the spreading dawn in the horizon.' This hadith, narrated by Muslim, serves as the primary textual evidence for defining the start of Fajr.
The hadith is ahad but accepted in the school.
4
Understand Dhuhr end time condition
Dhuhr ends when the shadow of any object becomes twice its height (excluding the shadow at zenith). This is a specific condition that distinguishes the Hanafi position from other schools.
Some other schools hold that Dhuhr ends when the shadow equals the object's height.

The Book of Prayer. Section on the times of prayer. The time for Fajr is from the horizontal dawn (true dawn) until sunrise, and for Dhuhr from its decline (zawal) until the shadow of everything becomes equal to twice its length, excluding the shadow of zenith. [Commentary: He said 'the Book of Prayer' meaning this is a book on the rulings of prayer and what pertains to it, and its precedence over other pillars because it is the most excellent and important. He said 'the time for Fajr' because time is the cause for the obligation of prayer as established in usul books, so he presented its discussion before other topics of prayer, and he presented the time of Fajr because it is the first prayer of the day and after waking up. His saying 'he guarded against' – know that dawn is of two types: the first is called false dawn, which is the whiteness that appears long in the sky from its horizon upwards like the tail of a wolf, then darkness follows it, then a horizontal light follows; i.e., spreading in the eastern directions of the sky, increasing gradually. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Do not let the elongated dawn deceive you, rather the dawn is the spreading dawn in the horizon' – narrated by Muslim and others. (1) Dhuha': with dammah, not declinable, a name for the sun without the definite article, you say: 'this is Dhuha' rising.' (2) It is called true dawn because it is more truthful in appearance than the elongated, and it is called the first dawn because it is the first light that appears like the tail of a wolf due to its thinness and elongation, and because the light is stronger at its top than its bottom, and false dawn because darkness follows it. As in the hashiya of al-Tahtawi on al-Durr al-Mukhtar. (3) In the wording: 'Do not let the adhan of Bilal or the elongated whiteness of the horizon prevent you from your suhur, until it becomes horizontal – i.e.'
Source pages p. 3

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the Book of Prayer from 'Umda al-Ri'aya', which is a commentary on 'Sharh al-Wiqaya'. The author begins by presenting the ruling on the times of Fajr and Dhuhr prayers. The matn (base text) states that the time for Fajr begins with 'al-subh al-mu'tarid' (the horizontal dawn, i.e., true dawn) and lasts until sunrise (طلوع ذكاء). The commentator explains that the phrase 'al-mu'tarid' distinguishes true dawn from 'al-mustatil' (the elongated dawn), which is the false dawn. The false dawn appears as a vertical pillar of light and is followed by darkness, while the true dawn spreads horizontally across the horizon. This distinction is crucial for determining the correct start of Fajr time. The time for Dhuhr begins at zawal (when the sun passes the meridian) and continues until the shadow of any object becomes twice its height, excluding the shadow at zenith. The commentary provides a hadith from Muslim to support the definition of true dawn. Key terms: 'ذكاء' means the sun; 'زوال' is the sun's decline; 'فيء الزوال' is the shadow at zenith which is excluded. The commentary also cites 'Sa'ayah' and 'Hashiyat al-Tahtawi' for further elaboration. This passage establishes the foundational rules for prayer times in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
الصبح المعترض (al-subh al-mu'tarid): The true dawn: light spreading horizontally across the eastern horizon.
الصبح الكاذب (al-subh al-kadhib): False dawn: a vertical beam of light appearing before true dawn, followed by darkness.
ذكاء (dhuha'): The sun, used as a proper noun without definite article.
زوال (zawal): The sun's passing of the meridian; beginning of Dhuhr time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hadith cited in this lesson, what kind of dawn should be used to determine the start of Fajr?
A) The elongated dawn (al-subh al-mustatil)
B) The horizontal dawn (al-subh al-mu'tarid)
C) Both dawns are acceptable
D) The dawn that appears brightest

Key Takeaway
Fajr begins at true horizontal dawn (not the false vertical dawn); Dhuhr begins at zawal and ends when shadows double.

The Time of Fajr: Distinguishing True Dawn from False Dawn
States the ruling for Fajr time and distinguishes true from false dawn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for the start of Fajr time and identify the distinguishing feature between true dawn (al-subh al-sadiq) and false dawn (al-subh al-kadhib) based on the hadith evidence.

Core Idea
Fajr begins when light spreads horizontally across the horizon, not at the vertical false dawn.

The opening of the Book of Prayer establishes that the time for Fajr is the horizontal dawn. The commentator explains that this is the true dawn, distinguished from the false dawn that appears vertically like a wolf's tail. A hadith warns against being deceived by the elongated false dawn. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly observing the Fajr prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage states the time for Fajr prayer begins 'from the horizontal dawn until sunrise'. This defines when the obligation of Fajr prayer takes effect.
2
Distinguish the two types of dawn
The commentator explains that dawn is of two types: false dawn (al-subh al-kadhib) appearing vertical and followed by darkness, and true dawn (al-subh al-sadiq) spreading horizontally.
3
Locate the dalil
The author cites the hadith: 'Do not let the elongated dawn deceive you, rather the dawn spreading in the horizon.' This is the proof for the ruling.
The hadith is narrated by Muslim and others.
4
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The operative phrase 'dawn spreading in the horizon' indicates that the true dawn is the horizontally spreading one. The prohibition of being deceived by the elongated dawn confirms that Fajr time begins only when the light spreads horizontally.
5
Note the consensus
This distinction is agreed upon by all schools; the false dawn does not trigger any prayer or fasting obligations.

Source Text

Book of Prayer. Chapter on the times of prayer. The time for Fajr is from the horizontal dawn until sunrise, and for Zuhr from its decline until the shadow of everything reaches twice its length except the shadow of the zenith. He said: avoid by the horizontal dawn the elongated one, which is the false dawn. (And for Zuhr from its decline until the shadow of everything reaches twice its length except the shadow of the zenith) There is need here to know the time of zawal. He said: The Book of Prayer; meaning this is a book on the rulings of prayer and what pertains to it, and its presentation before other pillars because it is the most virtuous and important. He said: The time for Fajr; since time is a cause for the obligation of prayer as established in the books of usul, he introduced its topics before other topics of prayer, and he introduced the time of Fajr because it is the first prayer of the day and after waking up. He said: avoid; know that the dawn is of two types: the first is called the false dawn, which is the whiteness that appears long in the sky from its horizon to the heights like a wolf's tail, then darkness follows, then a horizontally spreading light follows; meaning it spreads in the eastern sides of the sky and increases gradually. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Do not let the elongated dawn deceive you, rather the dawn spreading in the horizon.' Narrated by Muslim and others.
Source pages p. 3

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Prayer by establishing that prayer times are the cause of obligation. The matn states that Fajr time begins with the 'horizontal dawn' (al-subh al-mu'tarid). The commentator explains that this is the true dawn (al-sadiq), as opposed to the false dawn (al-kadhib) which appears vertically like a wolf's tail. The hadith explicitly warns against being deceived by the elongated false dawn. The legal significance is that Fajr prayer is not valid until the horizontal spread of light is observed. The author then transitions to the time for Zuhr, but the immediate commentary focuses solely on Fajr.

Key Terms
الصبح المعترض (al-subh al-mu'tarid): The dawn that spreads horizontally; the true dawn.
الصبح الكاذب (al-subh al-kadhib): The false dawn which appears vertically like a wolf's tail and is followed by darkness.
المستطيل (al-mustatil): The elongated false dawn.
المستطير (al-mustatir): The spreading dawn; the true dawn as used in the hadith.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The true dawn is horizontal; the false dawn is vertical.
C) The true dawn occurs before the false dawn.
D) The false dawn is followed by increased light.

Key Takeaway
Fajr time begins at the horizontally spreading true dawn, not the vertical false dawn; the hadith explicitly warns against the latter.

Determining Zawal for Zuhr: The Requirement of Level Ground
Method for determining zawal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for determining zawal and explain the three methods for leveling the ground described in the Hanafi tradition.

Core Idea
Accurate Zuhr timing requires perfectly level ground to observe the shadow's start; three practical methods are given.

Determining the start of Zuhr prayer depends on observing the sun's decline (zawal), which requires an accurate shadow measurement. The commentator outlines the essential condition of level ground and provides classical methods – from simple water tests to the surveyor's konya – to ensure precision.

Guided Path
1
Establish the legal context
Understand that Zuhr prayer time begins at zawal, the moment the sun passes its zenith. The shadow then starts to lengthen; observing this accurately requires a level surface.
2
Identify the condition
The text states that ground must be perfectly level – no height or depression – because the straightness of the shadow depends on it. This is the primary condition.
3
Examine method 1: water test
Pour water on the ground. If it flows equally in all directions, the ground is level. If it flows faster to one side, that side is lower.
Water naturally seeks low spots.
4
Examine method 2: rolling object test
Place a movable or rolling object (e.g., mercury, a ball). If it stays in place or rolls without bias, the ground is level. If it rolls to one side, that side is lower.
5
Examine method 3: konya instrument
Use an isosceles triangle (konya) with a plumb line (shaqul) hung from the apex. Place its base on the ground; if the plumb line aligns with the midpoint mark consistently when rotated, the ground is level.
The plumb line always hangs perpendicular to a level surface.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وطريقه ; أي طريقة عرفان الزوال، وفيء الزوال أن يجعل موضع من الأرض مستوياً بحيث لم يبق فيه ارتفاع ولا انخفاض، وإنما اشترط هذا لأنَّ استقامةً الظل لا يحصل إلا بها ولامتحان تسوية الأرض طرق : مستو، 6 . منها : أن يصب الماء هناك، فإن سال إلى جميع الجهات على السوية والمعية فهو ، وإن سال إلى بعض الأطرافِ بسرعة أو أكثر من الجانب الآخر علم أنه منخفض؛ لأنَّ الماء بطبعه مائل إلى المواضع المنخفضة، فيزالُ انخفاضه ويسوّى. ٢. ومنها : أن يوضع شيء مزحزح ; أي متحرك كالزئبق ، أو متدحرج كالبندقة ، فإن قام متحرجاً أو متدحرجاً عُلم أنه مستو، وإن مال إلى جانب عُلِمَ أنه غير مستو. . ومنها : أن يمتحن ذلك ببعض موازين المقننين من التقنية، وهم الذين يحفرون القناة، ويقال له بالفارسية: كاريز - ، وموازينهم آلاتهم التي يجربون بها تسوية الأرض وعدمها كَالكونيا بضم الكاف الفارسية، وهو مثلث متساوي الساقين يجعل في منتصف قاعدته علامة، ويُعلّق من رأسه وهو ملتقى الساقين شيء ثقيل يسمى بالشاقول. فإن كانت الأرض بحيث إذا أديرت القاعدة عليها إلى أي جهة كانت ; وقع الشاقول على العلامة فهي مستوية، وإلا لا، وذلك لأنه تقرر في مقره أن جميع الأثقال مائلة بالطبع إلى مركز العالم على سمتِ أعمدة ، وخط الشاقولُ عمود على سطح قاعدة الكونيا، وسطح قاعدته موازية على ذلك السطح المستوي، فيكون عموداً عليه أيضاً، فإن وقع الشاقول على المنتصف عُلم أنه مستوي، وإن مالَ يميناً أو شمالاً عُلِمَ أنه منخفض ومرتفع.
And its method is to level the ground so that none of its sides is raised and some lowered: either by pouring water, or by setting up the levels of the canal-diggers. He said: The way; meaning the method of knowing the zawal, and the shadow of the zenith is that one makes a place on the ground level so that there remains no height or depression. This condition is because straightness of the shadow is not obtained except by it. For testing the leveling of the ground there are methods: one of them is to pour water there; if it flows to all sides equally and together, it is level; if it flows to some sides quickly or more than the other side, it is known that it is low; because water by nature inclines to low places, so its low part is removed and leveled. A second method: to place something that moves, like mercury, or rolls like a ball; if it remains moving or rolling, it is known that it is level; if it inclines to a side, it is known that it is not level. Another method: to test it by some levels of the canal-diggers from technology, they are those who dig canals, called in Persian 'karez' – and their levels are their instruments with which they test the leveling of the ground, like the 'konya' (with damma of the Persian kaf), which is an isosceles triangle, at the midpoint of its base a mark is placed, and from its apex (where the legs meet) a heavy object called a plumb line is hung. If the ground is such that when the base is rotated on it to any direction, the plumb line falls on the mark, then it is level; otherwise not. This is because it is established in its place that all heavy objects naturally incline toward the center of the world along perpendiculars, and the line of the plumb line is perpendicular to the surface of the base of the konya, and the surface of its base is parallel to that level surface, so it is also perpendicular to it. If the plumb line falls on the middle, it is known that it is level; if it inclines right or left, it is known that it is low or high.
Source pages p. 4

Commentary
This passage explains how to determine the moment of zawal (the sun's noonday decline) for the start of Zuhr time. The key is that the ground must be perfectly level to obtain an accurate shadow measurement. The commentator outlines three practical methods: (1) pouring water to see if it spreads evenly; (2) placing a movable or rolling object to see if it stays stationary; (3) using a surveyor's instrument called a konya, which is a triangle with a plumb line to check horizontality. The legal relevance is that Zuhr time begins when the sun passes its zenith, which is known by observing the shadow's start to lengthen. These methods ensure accurate determination of zawal.

Key Terms
الزوال (al-zawal): The moment when the sun passes its zenith and begins to decline.
الشاقول (al-shaqul): A plumb line; a chord with a weight used to determine verticality.
الكونيا (al-kunya): A surveyor's instrument, an isosceles triangle with a plumb line to check horizontality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) To ensure the water flows evenly.
B) To obtain an accurate straight shadow for calculation.
C) To make the ground look nice.
D) To test the surveyor's instrument.

Key Takeaway
Determining the start of Zuhr requires level ground; the classical Hanafi texts provide practical methods to achieve this.

Three Methods for Testing Ground Levelness to Determine Zawal
Commentary on the method of recognizing zawal by leveling the ground

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three classical Hanafi methods for testing ground levelness and explain why level ground is necessary for determining the time of zawal.

Core Idea
Accurate zawal determination depends on perfectly level ground; three empirical methods ensure its reliability.

In fiqh, the time for zuhr prayer begins at zawal, when the sun passes its zenith. The shadow of a vertical object at that moment is the shortest of the day. But this observation is only reliable if the ground is perfectly level—any slope distorts the shadow. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains three practical methods used by classical scholars and surveyors to test levelness. By learning these, you will appreciate the precision required in Hanafi fiqh for prayer timings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Goal
The text begins by stating that the method of recognizing zawal requires level ground so the shadow is straight. Understand that tanpa level ground, the shadow is unreliable.
2
List the Three Methods
Read through the three methods: (1) pouring water and observing flow, (2) placing a moving or rolling object, (3) using the kūnyā instrument with a plumb line.
Each method relies on a different physical principle.
3
Analyze the First Two Methods
Water naturally flows to low areas, so if it flows evenly in all directions the ground is level; if it favours one side, that side is lower. Similarly, a rolling object will stop only on level ground; if it rolls, there is a slope.
These methods are simple and require no special tools.
4
Examine the Third Method in Detail
The kūnyā is an isosceles triangle with a mark at the midpoint of its base. A plumb line hangs from the apex. When the base is placed on the ground, if the plumb line falls on the mark, the ground is level; otherwise it is not. This works because the plumb line is always vertical, and the base is parallel to the surface; only when the surface is horizontal does the plumb line align with the midpoint.
This method is more precise and was used by tunnel diggers (kārez).
5
Connect to Fiqh
Recognize that the Hanafi school requires empirical verification for prayer times, not mere calculation. This shows the importance of direct observation and practical tools in classical fiqh.
The condition (shart) of level ground ensures the accuracy of the shadow observation.

Source Text

قوله : وطريقه ؛ أي طريقة عرفان الزوال، وفيء الزوال أن يجعل موضع من الأرض مستوياً بحيث لم يبق فيه ارتفاع ولا انخفاض، وإنما اشترط هذا لأنَّ استقامةً الظل لا يحصل إلا بها ولامتحان تسوية الأرض طرق :
١. منها : أن يصب الماء هناك، فإن سال إلى جميع الجهات على السوية والمعية فهو مستو، وإن سال إلى بعض الأطرافِ بسرعة أو أكثر من الجانب الآخر علم أنه منخفض؛ لأنَّ الماء بطبعه مائل إلى المواضع المنخفضة، فيزالُ انخفاضه ويسوّى.

٣. ومنها : أن يمتحن ذلك ببعض موازين المقننين من التقنية، وهم الذين يحفرون القناة، ويقال له بالفارسية: كاريز - ، وموازينهم آلاتهم التي يجربون بها تسوية الأرض وعدمها كالكُونيا بضم الكاف الفارسية، وهو مثلث متساوي الساقين يجعل في منتصف قاعدته علامة، ويُعلّق من رأسه وهو ملتقى الساقين شيء ثقيل يسمى بالشاقول.
فإن كانت الأرض بحيث إذا أديرت القاعدة عليها إلى أي جهة كانت ؛ وقع الشاقول على العلامة فهي مستوية، وإلا لا، وذلك لأنه تقرر في مقره أن جميع الأثقال مائلة بالطبع إلى مركز العالم على سمتِ أعمدة ، وخط الشاقولُ عمود على سطح قاعدة الكونيا، وسطح قاعدته موازية على ذلك السطح المستوي، فيكون عموداً عليه أيضاً، فإن وقع الشاقول على المنتصف عُلم أنه مستوي، وإن مالَ يميناً أو شمالاً عُلِمَ أنه منخفض ومرتفع.

He said: 'And its method' meaning the method of recognizing the zawal (sun's passing the meridian). And the shadow of zawal is that he makes a place of the ground level such that there remains no elevation or depression. This is required because the straightness of the shadow is only achieved by it. For testing the levelness of the ground there are methods: 1. To pour water there; if it flows to all directions equally and together then it is level; if it flows to some directions quickly or more from the other side, it is known to be depressed because water naturally inclines to low places, so its depression is removed and it is leveled. 2. To place a moving thing like mercury or a rolling thing like a bullet; if it stops moving or rolling it is known level; if it inclines to one side it is known not level. 3. To test it with some balances of the tunnellers from the technicians, who dig canal, and in Persian it is called 'kārez'. Their balances are their instruments with which they test levelness and unevenness, like the kūnyā (with a Persian kāf), which is an isosceles triangle with a mark at the midpoint of its base, and a heavy thing called a plumb line is hung from its apex where the two sides meet. If the ground is such that when the base is turned upon it to any direction, the plumb line falls on the mark, then it is level; otherwise not. This is because it is established that all heavy things naturally incline to the center of the world along vertical lines, and the plumb line is perpendicular to the surface of the base of the kūnyā, and the surface of its base is parallel to that level surface, so it is also perpendicular upon it. If the plumb line falls on the midpoint it is known level, if it deviates right or left it is known depressed and elevated. 'It spreads out and extends—like this...' And Hammād narrated: with his hands, he said: meaning horizontally. In Sahih Muslim (2:770) with his wording, and in Sahih Ibn Khuzaymah (3:210), and Jami' al-Tirmidhi (3:86).
Source pages p. 4

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn's phrase 'wa tariquhu' (and its method). The author explains that 'its method' refers to the way of recognizing the time of zawal (when the sun is at its zenith). The key prerequisite is that the ground be perfectly level—no bumps or depressions—because only then will the shadow be straight and reliable. He then details three practical methods for testing whether a piece of ground is truly level. The first is the water test: pour water and observe if it flows uniformly in all directions; if it flows faster or more on one side, that side is lower. The second uses a movable object like mercury or a ball: if it remains stationary, the ground is level; if it rolls, it is not. The third method employs a surveyor's instrument called the 'kūnyā' (a plumb-line level used by tunnel diggers). The kūnyā is an isosceles triangle with a mark at the midpoint of its base; a plumb line hangs from the apex. When the base is rotated on the ground, if the plumb line consistently falls on the midpoint, the ground is level. This is because the plumb always points to the center of the earth and is perpendicular to the base, so it aligns with the mark only when the surface is truly horizontal. The author then adds a brief hadith citation about spreading the hands, but this appears to be a cross-reference and does not directly relate to the leveling method. The focus remains on the precise steps needed to ensure an accurate observation of the shadow for determining prayer times.

Key Terms
زوال (zawāl): The time when the sun passes the meridian (its zenith); the moment after which shadows begin to lengthen from their shortest point.
ظل (ẓill): The shadow cast by a vertical object (like a gnomon). The straightness of the shadow indicates the levelness of the ground and helps determine zawal.
شاقول (shāqūl): A plumb line: a heavy weight suspended from a string used to find the vertical direction; in this text, it is part of the kūnyā instrument.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the plumb line always points to the center of the earth, and the base of the kūnyā is perpendicular to the plumb only when the surface is level.
B) Because the kūnyā is magnetic and aligns with the north pole.
C) Because the midpoint mark is placed exactly where the shadow falls.
D) Because the plumb line is heavier on one side.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi method for determining zawal requires perfectly level ground; three empirical tests (water, rolling object, and plumb-line level) ensure this condition is met.

Conditions of the Gnomon and the Indian Circle
Commentary on drawing the Indian circle and gnomon conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of the Indian circle and list the four conditions of the gnomon as required in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
The gnomon's length must be exactly one quarter of the circle's diameter to ensure precise prayer time determination.

Accurate prayer times are central to Islamic practice. The Hanafi school accepts traditional astronomical methods, such as the Indian circle and gnomon, as valid tools. This lesson explains what the Indian circle is and the four conditions the gnomon must meet for its shadow to be reliable according to the classical Hanafi commentary.

Guided Path
1
Define the Indian circle
Identify the Indian circle as a circle drawn on leveled ground, used by Hindu sages to observe shadows and determine prayer times. Note the condition that it must be entirely within the leveled area.
The circle's center is the gnomon's position.
2
Define the gnomon (miqyās)
The gnomon is a vertical indicator set at the circle's center. Lexically 'measure', technically a shadow-casting rod. Grasp its purpose.
Also called shākhis.
3
List the four conditions of the gnomon
Memorize the four prescribed conditions: conical shape, moderate thickness, sufficient weight (e.g., copper/iron), and length = one quarter of the circle's diameter.
The length condition ensures the shadow is shorter than the radius for unambiguous entry/exit.
4
Understand the rationale
The quarter-diameter length is derived from astronomical principles: the shadow should never exceed half the diameter, enabling clear distinction between shadow entry and exit.
This is known from books of astronomy (hay'a).

And upon it a circle is drawn, called the Indian circle, and in its center is erected a standing gnomon. He said: 'And upon it is drawn' meaning upon that leveled ground, on any center, a circle. It is a surface enclosed by a circular line completely; in its middle is a point from which all lines drawn to the circumference are equal. That line is called the circumference, and commonly the circle is referred to by it, and that point is called its center. It is required in drawing this circle that it does not reach the edges of the leveled area, so that one knows with certainty that the circle lies on the balanced surface, and thereby the entry and exit of the shadow is known without confusion. The first to derive this circle and base rulings upon it were the sages of India, hence it is called the Indian circle. He said: 'The Indian circle'; its form is as follows: [diagram labels]. He said: 'And is erected' meaning a gnomon is set up at the position of the center of that circle. Lexically it means measure; technically it is a raised indicator by which the shadow is known. Conditions are required in it: 1. It should be conical in shape. 2. It should be moderate between thin and thick. 3. It should have suitable heaviness to stay in place, such as being made of copper or iron. 4. Its length should be one quarter of the diameter of that circle, which is a straight line from the center to the circumference on both sides. This is stipulated although what is necessary is that it be such that its shadow is shorter than half the diameter of the circle, to distinguish its entry and exit, because the existence of the shadow (zill?) in most regions is not conceivable except within that, as detailed in books of astronomy.
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the matn, explaining the drawing of the Indian circle (al-dā'ira al-hindiyya) and the installation of the gnomon (miqyās). The author first clarifies that the circle should be drawn on the previously leveled ground. The circle is defined geometrically: it is a closed curve with a center point from which all radii are equal. A crucial condition is that the circle must be entirely within the leveled area, not reaching its edges, so that one is sure the entire circle lies on a level surface; this ensures accurate shadow observation. The circle is called 'Indian' because Hindu sages first devised it. The author then includes a diagram (labels listed). Next, he explains the gnomon: literally 'measure', technically a vertical indicator for shadows. Four conditions are given: (1) conical shape, (2) moderate thickness not too thin or thick, (3) heavy enough to stay fixed (e.g., made of copper or iron), (4) length equal to one quarter of the circle's diameter. The reason for this length is that the shadow should be shorter than half the diameter to avoid ambiguity when the shadow enters and exits the circle. This is detailed in astronomy books. The passage emphasizes precision in both the circle and the gnomon as necessary for correctly determining prayer times.

Key Terms
الدائرة الهندية (al-dā'ira al-hindiyya): A circle drawn on leveled ground to observe the shadow of a gnomon; used by Hindu sages to determine prayer times.
مقياس (miqyās): The vertical gnomon placed at the center of the Indian circle whose shadow indicates the time; must be conical and one quarter of the circle's diameter.
قطر الدائرة (quṭr al-dā'ira): The diameter: a straight line through the center connecting two points on the circumference; used to determine the gnomon's length.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) It must have a length equal to one quarter of the circle's diameter.
D) It must be of moderate thickness.

Key Takeaway
For a gnomon to be reliable in determining prayer times, it must satisfy four precise conditions: conical shape, moderate thickness, sufficient weight, and a length of one quarter of the circle's diameter.

The Indian Circle and Gnomon: A Hanafi Method for Determining Prayer Times
Description of the Indian Circle and conditions for the gnomon

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the construction and conditions of the Indian Circle (al-da'irah al-hindiyyah) and the gnomon (al-mikyas) as used in Hanafi fiqh for calculating prayer times.

Core Idea
A carefully leveled circle and precisely shaped gnomon allow Hanafi scholars to measure shadow length and determine prayer times with accuracy.

Determining the times of the five daily prayers is a foundational obligation in Islam. The Hanafi school, while relying on the general principles of shadow lengths, also developed a precise geometrical method using the 'Indian Circle' and a gnomon. This lesson unpacks the conditions and construction of this tool as explained in the classical commentaries, showing how Hanafi jurists integrated practical astronomy into fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the purpose of the Indian Circle
The circle is drawn on a leveled surface to track the gnomon's shadow. Its main function is to determine the entry and exit of the shadow relative to the circle, which indicates the sun's position for prayer times.
The circle must be fully within the leveled area to avoid measurement errors.
2
List the conditions for the gnomon (mikyas)
The gnomon must be conical, of moderate thickness, heavy enough to stay upright, and its height must be one quarter of the circle's diameter. These conditions ensure the shadow behaves predictably.
The proportion of height to diameter ensures the shadow tip falls within the circle for most of the day.
3
Explain how to verify the gnomon is vertical
Check that the distance from the gnomon's tip to three different points on the circumference is equal. This confirms the gnomon is perpendicular to the leveled surface.
This is a practical geometric test.
4
Describe the shadow behavior at different times
At sunrise, the shadow is long and its tip is outside the circle. As the sun rises, the shadow shortens until the tip enters the circle. The entry point is marked to record the time.
This process is used to determine the start of Zuhr time (when the shadow begins to decrease after zenith) and Asr time (when the shadow reaches a specific length).
5
Distinguish between the two types of shadow mentioned
The 'second shadow' is from a vertical gnomon (common for timekeeping); the 'first shadow' from a gnomon aligned with the sun. They are inversely related: the first shadow for a given altitude equals the second for the complementary altitude.
This relationship allows cross-checking measurements.

كذلك، بحيث يكون الأول لكل ارتفاع كالثاني، لتمام الارتفاع، وبالعكس. مثلاً إذا فرض ارتفاع الشمس عشرين درجة ، فالظل الأوّلُ المعكوس يكون بقدرِ الظل الثاني المستوي لارتفاع سبعين، والظل الثاني لارتفاع عشرين يكون مثل الظل الأول لارتفاع سبعين، فيتساوى الظل الأول والثاني في ثمن الدور. [٤]قوله : فتضع ؛ أي يجعل علامة على موضع دخول الظل عند وصوله إلى
Book of Prayer / Times of Prayer
And upon it a circle is drawn, called the 'Indian Circle', and in its center an upright gnomon is erected.
[1] His statement: 'and upon it a circle is drawn' means on that leveled ground, around any center a circle is drawn. It is a surface completely surrounded by a circular line, in its middle a point from which all lines drawn to the circumference are equal. That line is called the circumference of the circle, and often the circle itself is referred to by it. That point is called its center.
A condition for drawing this circle is that it should not reach the edges of the leveled area, so that it is known with certainty that the circle lies on the measured surface, and thus the entry and exit of the shadow can be known without confusion. The first to derive this circle and base rulings upon it were the sages of India, hence it is called the Indian Circle.
[2] His statement: 'the Indian Circle'; its form is as follows:
[Shadow at zenith, North point, Time of zenith, Arc, Entry of shadow, Center of circle, Exit of shadow, Top of gnomon, West point, Diameter of circle, Explanatory diagram of the Indian Circle, East point, This is the meridian line, South point]
As for his statement: 'and an upright gnomon is erected' means a gnomon is set up at the location of the center of that circle. Lexically it means measure; technically it is a raised pointer by which the shadow is known. Conditions for it include:
1. It should be conical in shape.
2. It should be moderate between thinness and thickness.
3. It should have sufficient weight to be fixed in place, such as being made of stone or iron.
4. Its length should be one quarter of the diameter of that circle. The diameter is a straight line passing from the center of the circle to its circumference on both sides. This condition is specified even though the requirement is that its shadow be shorter than half the diameter of the circle, to distinguish its entry and exit, because the existence of the shadow in most regions is not conceivable except within that, as detailed in books of astronomy.
So that the distance of its tip from three points on the circumference of the circle is equal, and let its height be a quarter of the circle's diameter. The tip of its shadow at the beginning of the day is outside the circle, but the shadow decreases until it enters the circle, so you place a mark at the entry point of the shadow on the circumference of the circle.
[1] His statement: 'so that' - when its distance from three points of the circumference on three sides of the circle is equal, then the distance is equal from all directions, so it is known that it stands straight without inclination.
[2] His statement: 'the tip of its shadow' means the shadow of that gnomon ends, and its tip is the separator between shadow and light. At the beginning of the day (i.e., at sunrise) it is outside that circle. Explanation: The shadow is sometimes taken from a gnomon erected perpendicular to the horizon plane, called the second shadow, the horizontal shadow, and the spread shadow; this is used in knowing the times. Sometimes it is taken from a gnomon set parallel to the horizon plane, perpendicular to a plane standing on the horizon and altitude circles, its tip facing the sun, like a peg standing on a board, moving according to the motion of the altitude circle, so that it always stands upon it. This shadow is called 'shadow' because it first occurs at the beginning of the day; also 'inverted' and 'reversed' because its tip is downward, and 'erect' because it stands on the horizon. At sunrise, the first shadow begins, and the second shadow is at its maximum length; then the first increases little by little as the sun rises above the horizon, and the second decreases correspondingly, such that the first for each altitude is like the second for the complementary altitude, and vice versa. For example, if the sun's altitude is assumed to be twenty degrees, the inverted first shadow equals the horizontal second shadow for an altitude of seventy, and the second shadow for an altitude of twenty equals the first shadow for an altitude of seventy; so the first and second shadows are equal at one-eighth of the revolution.
[3] His statement: 'decreases' means the shadow of that gnomon becomes decreasing and short as the sun rises until its tip enters that Indian Circle.
[4] His statement: 'you place' means he makes a mark at the entry point of the shadow when it reaches the circumference.
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory expansion (sharh) on the rules for determining prayer times using the Indian Circle. The text begins by defining the circle: a leveled surface with a drawn circle where a gnomon is placed at the center. The circle must be drawn entirely within the leveled area to avoid confusion about shadow entry and exit. The method originated from Indian astronomers, hence the name 'Indian Circle'. The commentary then details the conditions for the gnomon (mikyas): it should be conical, of moderate thickness, of sufficient weight to stay upright, and its height should be one quarter of the circle's diameter. This specific proportion ensures the shadow length stays within the circle at most times. The text explains how to verify the gnomon is vertical by checking equal distances from its tip to three points on the circumference. It describes shadow behavior: at sunrise the shadow is long and outside the circle; as the sun rises the shadow shortens until its tip enters the circle. The entry point is marked to determine time. The commentary distinguishes two types of shadow: the 'second/shadow' from a vertical gnomon (used for timekeeping) and the 'first shadow' from a gnomon aligned with the sun. The relationship between them is inverse. This detailed method was used by Hanafi scholars to accurately determine the times for zuhr (when shadow declines after zenith) and asr (when shadow length reaches specified ratios). The Hanafi school relies on this practical astronomy for prayer timing.

Key Terms
الدائرة الهندية (al-da'irah al-hindiyyah): A circle drawn on a leveled surface to measure shadow entry and exit for determining prayer times, invented by Indian scholars.
المقياس (al-mikyas): A gnomon or vertical pointer erected at the center of the circle to cast a shadow for time measurement.
الظل (al-zill): The shadow cast by the gnomon; its length and direction indicate the sun's position and thus prayer times.

Determining Qibla Direction Using the Indian Circle
Explains geometric method for finding qibla direction using shadows

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the steps of the Indian circle method for determining qibla direction and identify the two types of shadows involved.

Core Idea
The Indian circle method uses shadow progression and arc bisection to determine qibla direction.

This passage explains a precise geometric method for finding the direction of the qibla, a necessary condition for the validity of salah. It describes how to set up a gnomon, track its shadow on a drawn circle, and use the entry and exit points to derive the correct orientation.

Guided Path
1
Set up the gnomon
Ensure the gnomon is upright (equidistant from three points on the circumference) and its height is one quarter of the circle's diameter.
This guarantees the gnomon is perpendicular.
2
Mark shadow entry
At the beginning of the day, the shadow tip is outside the circle. As the sun rises, the shadow shortens and enters the circle. Place a mark at the entry point on the western side before midday.
Entry occurs before noon.
3
Mark shadow exit
After midday, the shadow lengthens and eventually exits the circle on the eastern side. Place a second mark at the exit point.
Exit occurs after noon.
4
Bisect the arc
The two marks define two arcs on the circle. Bisect the smaller or larger arc (whichever is between them) to find its midpoint.
Bisecting gives a point that, with the centre, aligns with qibla.
5
Draw the line
Draw a straight line from the bisection point through the centre of the circle. This line indicates the direction of the qibla.
A straight line has all points aligned without curvature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بأن يكون بُعْدُ رأسِهِ عن ثلاث نُقَط من محيط الدائرة متساوياً، ولتكن قامته بمقدار ربع قطر الدائرة. فرأس ظله" في أوائل النَّهار خارج عن الدائرة لكنَّ الظَّلَّ ينقص إلى أن يدخل في الدائرة ، فتضع علامة على مدخل الظل من محيط الدائرة [ ١ ] قوله : بأن يكون ؛ فإنّه إذا صارَ بعده عن ثلاث نقط من نقط المحيط التي في ثلاث جوانب الدائرة متساوياً كان البعد متساوياً من جميع الجهات، فيعلم أنه قائم على الاستقامة من دون ميلان. [٢] قوله : فرأس ظلّه ؛ أي ينتهي ظلّ ذلك المقياس، ورأسه الفاصل بين الظل والضوء في أول النهار - أي عند طلوع الشمس يكون خارج تلك الدائرة وتوضيحه : أن الظل قد يؤخذ من مقياس قائم عموداً على سطح الأفق ويسمى الظل الثاني والمستوي والمبسوط، وهذا هو المستعمل في معرفة الأوقات. وقد يؤخذ من مقياس منصوب على موازاة سطح الأفق ، عموداً على سطح قائم على دائرتي الأفق والارتفاع ، مواجها رأسه نحو الشمس : كوتد قائم على لوح ، يتحرك بحسب حركة دائرة الارتفاع، بحيث يقوم عليها أبداً، ويسمى هذا الظل ظلاً ؛ لأنَّ أوّل حدوثه في أوّل النهار ومعكوساً ومنكوساً ؛ لكون رأسه إلى تحت ، ومنصباً ؛ لكونه قائماً على الأفق. فعند طلوع الشمس يبتدئ الظل الأوّل، ويكون الظل الثاني في نهاية طوله، ثمّ لا يزال يتزايد الأول شيئاً فشيئاً بحسب ارتفاع الشمس من الأفق ، ويتناقص الثاني كذلك، بحيث يكون الأول لكل ارتفاع كالثاني، لتمام الارتفاع، وبالعكس. مثلاً إذا فرض ارتفاع الشمس عشرين درجة ، فالظل الأوّلُ المعكوس يكون بقدرِ الظل الثاني المستوي لارتفاع سبعين، والظل الثاني لارتفاع عشرين يكون مثل الظل الأول لارتفاع سبعين، فيتساوى الظل الأول والثاني في ثمن الدور. [٣] قوله : ينقص ؛ أي ظلّ ذلك المقياس يصير ناقصاً و قصيراً كلما ارتفعت الشمس إلى أن يدخل رأسه في تلك الدائرة الهندية. [٤]قوله : فتضع ؛ أي يجعل علامة على موضع دخول الظل عند وصوله إلى محيطها للدخول فيها من جانب المغرب قبل نصف النهار. [١] قوله : إلى حد ما ؛ فإنّ الظل لا يزالُ ينتقص ويقصر طولاً حسب ارتفاع الشمس إلى أن تصل الشمس إلى دائرة نصف النهار، وهي دائرة منصفة للفلكِ على نصفين، شرقي و غربي، مارةً شمالاً وجنوباً على قطبي العالم، وسمت الرأس والقدم. وحينئذ ينعدم الظل بالكلية إن وصلت الشمس على سمتِ الرأس، ثم يبدو منه شيء من جانب المشرق عند زوال الشمس عنها ؛ أي ميلانها عنها إلى المغرب، وإن لم تصل إلى سمت الرأس، بل تكون مائلة عنه إلى الجنوب مثلاً ، كما في أكثر بلاد الهند والسند، بل أكثر البلاد المعمورة يبقى قدر من الظل عند ذلك ، ثم عند زوال الشمس تلك الدائرة يشرعُ في الزيادة. (٢) قوله : ثم يزيد ؛ أي بعد وصوله إلى حد لا ينقص منه يشرع في الزيادة، فكلما النحطّت الشمس من نصف النهار إلى جانب المغرب طالَ ذلك الظل إلى الشرق، ولا يزالُ كذلك حتى ينتهي إلى محيط الدائرة الهندية، ثم يخرج منها، ولا يزال يطولُ إلى أن يغرب الشمس. [۳] قوله : القوس ؛ هو عبارة عن قطعة محيط الدائرة، يعني إذا وضعت علامة على مدخل الظـل من جانب الغرب، وعلى مخرج الظل من جانب الشرق، فيكون قوسان من قسمي محيط الدائرة الهندية محصورين بينهما بالضرورة ؛ أحدهما أصغر من النصف، والآخر أكبر من النصف، فننصفُ القوس التي بينهما ؛ أي قوس كان منهما ؛ أي تقطع بقطعتين متساويتين. [٤]قوله : خطا مستقيماً ؛ هو الخط الذي يكون وضعه بحيث يتقابل ؛ أي نقطة تفرض عليه بعضها لبعض ؛ أي لا يكون بعضهما مرتفعاً، وبعضها منخفضاً، وما لا يكون كذلك يُسمى خطاً مُنحنياً، ومنه محيط الدائرة.
Such that the distance of its head from three points of the circumference is equal, and its height is one quarter of the circle's diameter. The head of its shadow at the beginning of the day is outside the circle, but the shadow decreases until it enters the circle. So you place a mark at the shadow's entry point on the circumference. [1] His statement: 'such that' – because when its distance from three points of the circumference on three sides of the circle becomes equal, then the distance is equal from all sides, so it is known that it is erect without inclination. [2] His statement: 'the head of its shadow' – meaning the shadow of that measure ends, and its head is the separator between shadow and light at the beginning of the day – i.e., at sunrise it is outside that circle. Explanation: The shadow may be taken from a measure erect perpendicular to the horizon, called the second, plane, and extended shadow; this is used for knowing the times. And it may be taken from a measure set parallel to the horizon, perpendicular to the plane erect on the horizon and altitude circles, facing its head toward the sun: like a peg erect on a board, moving according to the motion of the altitude circle, always standing on it. This shadow is called 'shadow' because its first occurrence is at the beginning of the day; and inverted and reversed because its head is downward; and perpendicular because it stands on the horizon. At sunrise, the first shadow begins, and the second shadow is at its maximum length. Then the first gradually increases according to the sun's altitude from the horizon, and the second decreases likewise, such that the first for each altitude equals the second for the complementary altitude, and vice versa. For example, if the sun's altitude is twenty degrees, the first inverted shadow equals the second plane shadow for an altitude of seventy, and the second shadow for twenty equals the first shadow for seventy. Thus the first and second shadows are equal at eighth of the rotation. [3] His statement: 'decreases' – meaning that shadow of that measure becomes less and shorter as the sun rises until its head enters that Indian circle. [4] His statement: 'you place' – meaning you make a mark at the shadow's entry point when it reaches the circumference to enter it from the western side before midday. [1] His statement: 'to a certain limit' – because the shadow continues to decrease and shorten in length according to the sun's altitude until the sun reaches the midday circle, which is the circle dividing the sphere into two halves, eastern and western, passing north and south through the poles of the world, and the zenith and nadir. At that point the shadow disappears completely if the sun reaches the zenith, then something appears from the eastern side when the sun leaves it – i.e., its inclination from it to the west. If it does not reach the zenith but is inclined from it to the south, for example, as in most of India and Sindh – indeed most inhabited lands – some shadow remains at that time. Then when the sun leaves that circle, it begins to increase. (2) His statement: 'then it increases' – meaning after reaching the limit from which it does not decrease, it begins to increase. As the sun descends from midday to the west, that shadow lengthens to the east, and continues until it reaches the circumference of the Indian circle, then exits it, and continues to lengthen until sunset. [3] His statement: 'the arc' – it is a segment of the circle's circumference. That is, when you place a mark at the shadow's entry on the western side and a mark at the shadow's exit on the eastern side, there are necessarily two arcs between them from the two parts of the Indian circle's circumference: one less than half and the other greater than half. So we bisect the arc between them – whichever arc – i.e., we cut it into two equal parts. [4] His statement: 'a straight line' – it is the line whose position is such that points on it face each other – i.e., no part is raised and another lowered. What is not like that is called a curved line, like the circumference of a circle.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the ruling regarding the method of determining the direction of the qibla using an Indian circle. The author explains the geometric procedure to ensure the gnomon is upright and to mark shadow positions. First, the gnomon must be such that its tip is equidistant from three points on the circumference, and its height is one quarter of the circle's diameter. At the start of the day, the shadow tip falls outside the circle; it then shortens until it enters the circle. A mark is placed at the entry point on the western side before midday. The commentary distinguishes between two types of shadows: the second/plane/ extended shadow (used for prayer times) and the first/inverted/reversed shadow. At sunrise, the first shadow begins and the second is at its maximum; they behave inversely. As the sun rises, the first shadow increases and the second decreases until the sun reaches the midday circle. At that point, if the sun is at the zenith, the shadow disappears; otherwise some shadow remains. After midday, the shadow begins to lengthen again, eventually exiting the Indian circle. The shadow entry (west) and exit (east) points define two arcs; the arc between them is bisected. A straight line drawn from the bisection point through the centre of the circle gives the direction of the qibla. The commentary explains that a straight line is one where any point on it is aligned with another; a curved line, like the circumference, is not straight.

Key Terms
الظل الأول (al-zill al-awwal): The shadow cast by a gnomon placed facing the sun, called inverted shadow; its length increases from sunrise to midday, then decreases.
دائرة هندية (da'ira hindiyya): A circle drawn on the ground, typically of a certain size, used to track the shadow of a gnomon to find the qibla direction.
نصف النهار (nisf al-nahar): The midday circle, the line dividing the celestial sphere into eastern and western halves; when the sun crosses it, shadows are minimal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Determining the Meridian Line for Noon Prayer Time
Explains the astronomical method to find the meridian line for determining noon

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the astronomical method for determining the meridian line and explain why it indicates the time of noon for Zuhr prayer.

Core Idea
The meridian line, found by bisecting the arc of shadow entry and exit, marks the exact noon when the sun is at its zenith.

You know that Zuhr prayer must be offered after the sun passes its zenith. But how do you know exactly when that is? This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya explains a practical geometric method using a gnomon and a circle to find the meridian line. Once you understand this, you'll see how the Hanafi school relies on precise astronomical observation to determine prayer times.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem
We need to find the exact moment of noon for the Zuhr prayer. The sun's shadow behaves predictably: it shortens then lengthens.
The method uses symmetry of the sun's path around noon.
2
Observe shadow behaviour
Place a vertical gnomon. Draw a circle around it. Mark where the shadow tip touches the circle before noon (entry) and after noon (exit).
The entry and exit points are on opposite sides of the meridian.
3
Locate entry and exit points
These two points lie on the circumference of the circle. The arc between them is symmetric about the north-south line.
4
Bisect the arc
Find the midpoint of the arc between entry and exit. This point corresponds to the direction of the meridian.
5
Draw the meridian line
Draw a straight line from the midpoint to the center of the circle and extend it to the opposite side. This line is the meridian. When the shadow falls on it, it is noon.
The line passes through the north-south axis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصلاة/ أوقات الصلاة
[٢]
ولا شك أن الظَّلَّ ينقص إلى حد ما، ثُمَّ يزيد إلى أن ينتهي إلى محيط الدائرة ، ثُمَّ يخرج منها، وذلك بعد نصف النهار، فتضعُ علامة على مخرج الظَّلَّ، فَتُنَصفُ القوس التي ما بين مدخل الظل ومخرجه ، وترسم خطا مستقيما من منتصف القوس إلى مركز الدائرة ، مُخْرَجاً إلى الطرف الآخر من المحيط، فهذا الخط، هو خطّ نصف النهار"، فإذا كان ظل المقياس على هذا الخط ، فهو نِصْفُ النَّهار [1]قوله : إلى مركز الدائرة ؛ يعني ترسم خطاً مُستقيماً من منتصف تلك القوس، منتهياً إلى مركز الدائرة الهندية مخرجاً إلى الطرف الآخر المقابل لمبدأ ذلك الخط. [٢] قوله : هو . خط نصف النهار ؛ سُمِّي به ؛ لأنه في سطح دائرة نصف النهار، أو لأنه إذا وصل الظل عليه ينتصف النهار ؛ وذلك لأنَّ الظل أبداً يكون في سطح دائرة الارتفاع، والدائرة الهندية مركزها مركز الأفق الحسي، فمخرج الظل ومدخله يكون بمنزلة تقاطع دائرتي الارتفاع والأفق، وهذا التقاطع يسمى نقطة السمت. وبعد نقطتي السمتِ عن نقطتي الشمال والجنوب، وهما نقطتا تقاطع نصف النهار والأفق متساويان، فمنتصفا القوس التي بين مدخل الظل ومخرجه بمنزلة نقطتي الشمال والجنوب، فإذا نصفّت تلك القوس وأخرج من منتصفه خط مستقيم، وقع دائرة نصف النهار لا محالة، ودلائل هذه المقدمات واضحة لمن له مهارة في علم الهيأة.
[٣]قوله : فهو ؛ أي فذلك الوقت وقت نصف النهار، فإنّه إذا كان ذلك الخط في سطح دائرة نصف النهار، فإذا وصل الظل إليه عُلِمَ أنّ الشمس وصلت إلى دائرة نصف النهار، فيكون ذلك الوقت وقت نصف النهار.
واعلم أنه قد يستخرج خط مستقيم آخر من منتصفي القوسين اللتين تميزتا بخطِ نصف النهار مار بمركز الدائرة، ويسمّى خط الاعتدال ، وخط المشرق والمغرب، فتنقسم الدائرة بهذين الخطين أربعة أقسام متساوية، ثم يقسّم كل قسم منها تسعين جزءاً متساوية ؛ للاحتياج إليها في بعض الأعمال كاستخراج سمت القبلة ونحوه.
قال قاضي زاده الرومي في «شرح ملخص الجغميني»: «اعلم أن لاستخراج هذين الخطين مسالك أخرى إلا أن الأشهر هو المسلك المذكور، ولا شك أنه مبني على كون الشمس حين وصول رأس الظل إلى محيط الدائرةِ قبل الزوال وبعده على مدار واحد من المدارات اليومية الموازية لمعدل النهار، وليس كذلك في الحقيقة.
Book of Prayer / Times of Prayer [2] And there is no doubt that the shadow decreases to a certain limit, then increases until it reaches the circumference of the circle, then emerges from it, and that is after noon. So you place a mark on the exit of the shadow, then you bisect the arc that is between the entry of the shadow and its exit, and you draw a straight line from the midpoint of the arc to the center of the circle, extending to the other side of the circumference. This line is the meridian line. So if the shadow of the gnomon is on this line, then it is noon. [1] His statement: 'to the center of the circle'; meaning you draw a straight line from the midpoint of that arc, ending at the center of the Indian circle, extending to the opposite side opposite the beginning of that line. [2] His statement: 'it is the meridian line'; it is named that because it is on the surface of the meridian circle, or because when the shadow reaches it, the noon is halfway; that is because the shadow is always on the surface of the altitude circle, and the Indian circle has its center at the center of the sensory horizon. The exit and entry of the shadow are like the intersection of the altitude circle and the horizon, and this intersection is called the zenith point. And the distance of the two zenith points from the north and south points, which are the intersection points of the meridian and the horizon, are equal. So the midpoints of the arc between the entry and exit of the shadow are like the north and south points. So if that arc is bisected and a straight line is drawn from its midpoint, it certainly falls on the meridian circle. The proofs of these premises are clear to one skilled in astronomy. [3] His statement: 'it is'; meaning that time is the time of noon. Because if that line is on the surface of the meridian circle, then when the shadow reaches it, it is known that the sun has reached the meridian circle, so that time is the time of noon. And know that another straight line may be extracted from the midpoints of the two arcs that were distinguished by the meridian line, passing through the center of the circle, and it is called the equinox line, and the line of east and west. So the circle is divided by these two lines into four equal sections. Then each section is divided into ninety equal parts; due to the need for them in some tasks such as extracting the direction of the qibla and the like. Qadi Zada al-Rumi said in his commentary on 'Talkhis al-Jighmini': 'Know that there are other methods for extracting these two lines, but the most famous is the mentioned method. There is no doubt that it is based on the sun, when the tip of the shadow reaches the circumference of the circle before and after zenith, being on one parallel of the daily parallels parallel to the celestial equator, but in reality it is not so.'
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the method to determine the meridian line (خط نصف النهار) in order to ascertain the time of noon (نصف النهار) for the noon prayer (صلاة الظهر). It expands on the base text of al-Wiqaya. The commentator describes the astronomical procedure: as the sun rises, the shadow decreases until a certain point (when the sun is at its maximum altitude), then after noon (actually after zenith) it increases again. The shadow reaches the circumference of a circle (likely the horizon circle or an artificial circle drawn around the gnomon) at two points: one before noon (entry) and one after noon (exit). The arc between these two points on the circumference is bisected, and a straight line is drawn from the midpoint to the center of the circle and extended to the opposite side. This line is the meridian line. When the shadow of the gnomon falls on this line, it is exactly noon. The commentator then clarifies terms: 'Indian circle' refers to the horizon circle (دائرة الأفق الحسي). The intersection of the altitude circle (دائرة الارتفاع) and the horizon is called the zenith point (نقطة السمت). The midpoint of the arc between the two shadow positions corresponds to the north-south direction. Therefore, the line drawn from that midpoint through the center coincides with the meridian. The commentator also mentions that another perpendicular line (the equinox line) can be drawn from the midpoints of the two arcs created by the meridian line, dividing the circle into quadrants, which are further subdivided into 90 degrees for calculations like determining the qibla direction. Finally, a quote from Qadi Zada al-Rumi notes that this method assumes the sun's daily path is parallel to the celestial equator, which is a simplification, but it is the famous method.

Key Terms
خط نصف النهار (khatt nisf al-nahar): The meridian line; the line drawn from north to south through the zenith, used to determine the time of noon when the shadow aligns with it.
الدائرة الهندية (al-da'ira al-hindiyya): The 'Indian circle' refers to the horizon circle (دائرة الأفق) centered at the observer's location, used for shadow observations.
نقطة السمت (nuqtat al-samt): The zenith point; the point on the horizon circle where the altitude circle intersects it, corresponding to the sun's azimuth at the time of entry/exit of the shadow.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how is the meridian line (خط نصف النهار) established?
A) By drawing a line from the center of the circle to the point of sunrise.
B) By bisecting the arc between the entry and exit points of the shadow and drawing a line from the midpoint to the center of the circle.
C) By observing when the shadow is shortest and marking that direction.
D) By connecting the north and south points of the horizon directly.

Key Takeaway
The meridian line is found by bisecting the arc of shadow entry and exit and drawing a line from the midpoint through the center; this line indicates solar noon for Zuhr prayer.

Determining the Meridian Line for Noon Prayer Time
Technical description of astronomical timekeeping for prayer times.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of the meridian line, explain the astronomical method for its determination, and apply the conditions for accurate observation to ensure correct Zuhr time.

Core Idea
The meridian line is the north-south line that, when the shadow falls on it, marks the sun's zenith and the beginning of Zuhr time.

This lesson explains the classical Hanafi method for determining the meridian line (khatt nisf al-nahar) using a gnomon and circle. This knowledge is essential for accurately establishing the beginning of Zuhr prayer time. The passage, from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, provides both geometric reasoning and practical conditions for reliable observation, drawing on astronomy and timekeeping principles.

Guided Path
1
Understand the geometric construction
The meridian line is found by drawing a straight line from the midpoint of the arc between the entry and exit points of a shadow on a circle, through the center to the opposite side. This line represents the north-south direction.
The 'Indian circle' is a circle drawn on the ground with the gnomon at its center.
2
Identify the conditions for accurate observation
The text lists several conditions: perform the observation near the summer solstice, in clear weather, avoid times when the sun is near the horizon or near noon, and ensure the gnomon is vertical.
These conditions minimize errors due to solar declination changes and shadow dispersion.
3
Apply the alternative methods
The passage presents three alternative methods: (1) altitude method – measure altitude before and after noon, bisect the angle between two shadow lines; (2) shadow length method – mark shadow tips of equal length before and after noon, draw perpendicular bisector; (3) sunrise/sunset method – draw shadow lines at sunrise and sunset, bisect the angle.
These methods are more robust against the assumption of parallel daily arcs.
4
Distinguish fay' and zill
Linguistically, fay' (used for shadow after noon) and zill (before noon) are distinguished; the shadow at noon is called fay' al-zawal due to its connection to the zawal (passing of the sun).
This distinction is important for Asr time calculation, which uses the length of fay' after noon.

وبعد نقطتي السمتِ عن نقطتي الشمال والجنوب، وهما نقطتا تقاطع نصف النهار والأفق متساويان، فمنتصفا القوس التي بين مدخل الظل ومخرجه بمنزلة نقطتي الشمال والجنوب، فإذا نصفّت تلك القوس وأخرج من منتصفه خط مستقيم، وقع دائرة نصف النهار لا محالة، ودلائل هذه المقدمات واضحة لمن له مهارة ذلك الخط تحت في علم الهيأة.
[٣]قوله : فهو ؛ أي فذلك الوقت وقت نصف النهار، فإنّه إذا كان ذلك الخط في سطح دائرة نصف النهار، فإذا وصل الظل إليه عُلِمَ أنّ الشمس وصلت إلى دائرة نصف النهار، فيكون ذلك الوقت وقت نصف النهار.
واعلم أنه قد يستخرج خط مستقيم آخر من منتصفي القوسين اللتين تميزتا بخطِ نصف النهار مار بمركز الدائرة، ويسمّى خط الاعتدال ، وخط المشرق والمغرب، فتنقسم الدائرة بهذين الخطين أربعة أقسام متساوية، ثم يقسّم كل قسم منها تسعين جزءاً متساوية ؛ للاحتياج إليها في بعض الأعمال كاستخراج سمت القبلة ونحوه.
قال قاضي زاده الرومي في «شرح ملخص الجغميني»: «اعلم أن لاستخراج هذين الخطين مسالك أخرى إلا أن الأشهر هو المسلك المذكور، ولا شك أنه مبني على كون الشمس حين وصول رأس الظل إلى محيط الدائرةِ قبل الزوال وبعده على مدار واحد من المدارات اليومية الموازية لمعدل النهار، وليس كذلك في الحقيقة.

والظُّلُ الذي في هذا الوقت هو في الزَّوال ، فإذا زالَ الظَّلُ من هذا الخطّ إذن ينبغي أن تراعى عدة أمور ليقرب العمل من التحقيق، كأن يكون الشمس في الانقلاب الصيفي أو قريباً منه لبطء حركة الميل المخل بالموازاة هناك، وكون الظل أبينُ في الصيف لصفاء الهواء وشدة الشعاعِ وقلّة عوارض الجو المانعة من أخذ الظل. وأن لا تكون قريبة من الأفق ؛ إذ لا يتحقق أطرافُ الظل عند ذلك لتشتتها، ولا من نصف النهار ؛ لبطوء تقلّص الظلّ هناك وانبساطه ، فلا يتعين وقت الدخولُ والخروج. فإذا روعي هذه الشرائط تحفظ الموازاة بقدر الإمكان، ويتبين الظل، ويسلم عن تشتت طرفه وبطوء حركته». انتهى.
وذكر شراح تشريح الأفلاك وغيرهم أنّ من مسالك استخراج الخطين المذكورين أن ي ن يخرج من قاعدة المقياس خط مستقيم على استقامة الظل قبل نصف النهار، ويؤخذ الارتفاع في تلك الحالة، ثم ينظر بعد نصف النهار فإذا صار الارتفاعُ مثل الارتفاع الأوّل يخرجُ من القاعدة خط آخر على استقامة الظل، فيحصل في الأغلب زاوية، وتنصف تلك الزاوية، فالخط المنصف . هو خط نصف النهار. ومنها : أن يرصد الظل للمقياس قبل نصف النهار، ويُعَلِّمُ على رأسه علامة، ثمّ يرصد الظل بعد نصف النهار إلى أن صار مثل الظلّ الأوّل، ويُعَلِّمُ على رأسه علامة، ويوصل بين العلامتين بخط مستقيم، ويقام على ذلك الخط عمود، فهو خط نصف النهار.
ومنها : أن يستخرج خطّ على امتدادِ ظلّ المقياس عند طلوع الشمس وغروبها في يوم واحد بخطين، وتنصفُ الزاوية الواقعة بينهما فهو خط نصف النهار.
[1] قوله : هو فيء الزوال ؛ الفيء في اللغة بمعنى الرجوع، وهو من أسماء الظل مطلقاً، سمي به لرجوعِهِ من جانب إلى جانب، ومنهم من يخصه بما بعد الزوال، وما قبل الزوال يخص باسم الظلّ، وهذا الظلّ الذي هو عند استواء الشمس على نصف النهار، واستواء ظل المقياس على خط نصف النهار إنما سمي بفيء الزوال ؛ لأنّ الزوال متّصل به ، فإضافة الفيء إلى الزوال لأدنى ملابسته.
The pillar of care upon the explanation of al-Wiqayah. [3] To the center of the circle, extending to the other side of the circumference, so this line is the meridian line. If the shadow of the gnomon is on this line, it is noon. [1] His statement: to the center of the circle; meaning you draw a straight line from the midpoint of that arc, ending at the center of the Indian circle, extending to the other side opposite the start of that line. [2] His statement: it is the meridian line; it is so named because it lies in the plane of the meridian circle, or because when the shadow reaches it, noon occurs. This is because the shadow is always in the plane of the altitude circle, and the Indian circle has its center at the center of the sensible horizon, so the exit and entry of the shadow are like the intersection of the altitude and horizon circles, and this intersection is called the zenith point. The distances of the zenith points from the north and south points (which are the intersections of the meridian and horizon) are equal, so the midpoints of the arc between the entry and exit of the shadow correspond to the north and south points. Thus, if that arc is bisected and a straight line is drawn from its midpoint, it inevitably falls on the meridian circle. The proofs of these premises are clear to one skilled in astronomy. [3] His statement: so it; meaning that time is the time of noon. For if that line is in the plane of the meridian circle, then when the shadow reaches it, it is known that the sun has reached the meridian circle, so that time is the time of noon. Know that another straight line may be drawn from the midpoints of the two arcs that are distinguished by the meridian line, passing through the center of the circle, and it is called the equinoctial line, and the east-west line. Thus the circle is divided by these two lines into four equal parts, then each part is divided into ninety equal parts for use in some operations such as extracting the direction of the qibla and the like. Qadi Zadeh al-Rumi said in his commentary on the Mulakhkhas al-Jighmini: 'Know that there are other methods for extracting these two lines, but the most famous is the mentioned method. There is no doubt that it is based on the fact that the sun, when the tip of the shadow reaches the circumference of the circle before and after noon, follows a single daily path parallel to the celestial equator, but this is not true in reality.' The Book of Prayer / Times of Prayer. The shadow at this time is the shadow of midday (al-zawal). If the shadow moves from this line, then several matters should be observed to bring the work closer to accuracy: such as the sun being in the summer solstice or near it because of the slow movement of the inclination that disrupts parallelism there, and that the shadow is clearer in summer due to the purity of the air, intensity of rays, and few atmospheric obstacles preventing the shadow from being taken. That it should not be near the horizon, because the edges of the shadow are not discernible there due to their dispersion, nor near noon, because of the slowness of the shadow's contraction and expansion there, so the time of entry and exit is not determined. If these conditions are observed, parallelism is preserved as much as possible, the shadow becomes clear, and it is safe from the dispersion of its edge and slow movement.' End. The commentators on Tashrih al-Aflak and others mentioned that among the methods for extracting the two mentioned lines is that a straight line is drawn from the base of the gnomon along the direction of the shadow before noon, and the altitude is taken at that moment. Then after noon, when the altitude becomes equal to the first altitude, another line is drawn from the base along the direction of the shadow, resulting in an angle in most cases, and that angle is bisected; the bisecting line is the meridian line. Another method: one observes the shadow of the gnomon before noon and marks its tip, then observes the shadow after noon until it becomes equal to the first shadow, and marks its tip. Then a straight line is drawn between the two marks, and a perpendicular is erected on that line; that is the meridian line. Another method: one draws a line along the extension of the shadow of the gnomon at sunrise and sunset on the same day, resulting in two lines, and the angle between them is bisected; that is the meridian line. [1] His statement: it is the shadow of midday (fay' al-zawal). Linguistically, fay' means return, and it is a name for absolute shadow, so named because it returns from one side to another. Some restrict it to after noon, and what is before noon is specifically called zill. This shadow, which occurs when the sun is directly overhead at noon and the shadow of the gnomon is on the meridian line, is called fay' al-zawal because the noon (zawal) is connected to it, so the attribution of fay' to zawal is due to the slightest connection.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage is a technical commentary (sharh) on the text of al-Wiqayah, explaining how to determine the meridian line (khatt nisf al-nahar) and the shadow at noon (fay' al-zawal) for the purpose of establishing the time of Zuhr prayer. The author begins by describing the geometric method: a line is drawn from the midpoint of the arc between the entrance and exit points of the shadow on a circle, extending through the center to the opposite side. This line is the meridian line. When the shadow of a gnomon (a vertical stick) falls exactly on this line, it indicates that the sun has reached the meridian and it is the time of noon (zawal). The text then explains the reasoning: the shadow always lies in the plane of the altitude circle, and the intersection of the altitude and horizon circles gives the zenith point. By bisecting the arc between the shadow positions, one obtains the north-south line, which is the meridian. The passage further discusses the equinoctial line (khatt al-i'tidal) which is perpendicular to the meridian, dividing the circle into four quadrants for further calculations like determining the qibla direction. A quote from Qadi Zadeh al-Rumi critiques the assumption of parallelism used in the simplest method, noting that more accurate methods exist. The text then shifts to practical conditions for accuracy: the observation should be made near the summer solstice when the sun's declination changes slowly, in clear weather, and avoiding times when the shadow is too short (near noon) or too long (near horizon). It then lists three alternative methods: (1) observing the altitude before and after noon and bisecting the angle between two shadow lines; (2) marking the tip of the shadow at equal lengths before and after noon and drawing the perpendicular bisector; (3) using sunrise and sunset shadows. Finally, a footnote defines 'fay' al-zawal' linguistically: fay' means return, and it is used for the shadow after noon, while zill is used before noon. The shadow at noon is called fay' al-zawal because it is associated with the moment of zawal (passing of the sun over the meridian). This entire discussion is essential for the correct determination of the time for the Zuhr prayer, which begins when the sun has passed its zenith and the shadow starts to lengthen.

Key Terms
خط نصف النهار (khatt nisf al-nahar): The meridian line, a straight line running north-south on the horizontal plane; when the shadow of a gnomon falls on it, it indicates the sun is at its zenith and it is the time of noon.
فيء الزوال (fay' al-zawal): The shadow cast at the moment of noon when the sun is at its meridian; it is the slightest shadow (or no shadow at the equator) and is used as a reference for defining the beginning and end of the Zuhr time.
المقياس (al-miqyas): A gnomon or vertical stick used to measure shadows; its base is the point from which shadow lines are drawn to determine the meridian.

Key Takeaway
Accurate determination of the meridian line through astronomical observation ensures that Zuhr prayer begins precisely when the sun has passed its zenith, a requirement in Hanafi fiqh.

Determining Zuhr Time: The Concept of Fay' al-Zawal
Defines fay' al-zawal and explains Zuhr time determination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define fay' al-zawal, explain how Zuhr prayer time is determined according to the Hanafi school, and reconcile the apparent disagreement among Hanafi jurists regarding the start of Zuhr.

Core Idea
Zuhr time begins not at exact decline, but when the shadow perceptibly increases after its shortest length.

This passage defines the key term 'fay' al-zawal' and explains the practical determination of the start of Zuhr prayer. It resolves an ambiguity among Hanafi scholars by distinguishing between the actual astronomical event and the sensory evidence upon which Shariah obligations are based. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly observing prayer times in different seasons and latitudes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its linguistic meaning
The term 'fay' al-zawal' is defined linguistically and technically. Note that 'fay'' means shadow in general but is sometimes restricted to post-noon shadow. Understand why it is attached to 'zawal' (decline) even though it occurs at the moment of alignment.
Linguistic analysis helps clarify the technical usage in fiqh.
2
Distinguish between actual and perceptible decline
The passage explains that actual decline (the moment the sun passes the meridian) is imperceptible. What is perceptible is the subsequent increase in shadow length. Shariah obligations are linked to what is perceptible.
This distinction is central to the Hanafi position.
3
Apply the principle to prayer time
Zuhr time begins when the shadow increases after its shortest length. The shortest shadow is called fay' al-zawal. The increase is the sign of entry of time.
In most regions some shadow remains at noon, so the increase is from that baseline.
4
Reconcile the intra-Hanafi disagreement
Some Hanafi jurists said Zuhr begins at the first moment of decline; others said after a minute. The passage resolves this by noting that the first group meant actual decline (divine knowledge) and the second meant perceptible decline (sensory evidence). Both agree on the operative ruling.
Understanding the resolution prevents confusion in practice.
